The Two Types of Qudra (Power)
Maturidi divides qudra into two types and states one power suffices for obedience and disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's division of qudra (power) into two types and the doctrine that one power suffices for obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
One divine power suffices for opposite actions — a unique Maturidi position.

Imam Maturidi, following Abu Hanifa, presents a nuanced view of human power. He distinguishes between preparatory power (sound causes and instruments) and the actual power that accompanies the act. This lesson explores that division and its implications for human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own wording guide your understanding.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two types of power
The passage explicitly divides qudra into two types. Locate the description of each. What distinguishes them?
One type is preparatory, the other is concomitant with the act.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi states that one power suffices for obedience and disobedience. Reflect on why this is a significant theological claim.
This challenges the idea that different acts require different powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأصل عنده في المسمى باسم القدرة التي بها يتحقق الفعل أنها على قسمين أحدهما هي التي تجهز الأرضية لها بسلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات والثاني هي معنى لا يقدر على تبيين حده بشيئ سوى أنه ليس إلا للفعل لا يجوز وجوده بحال إلا ويقع به الفعل معه عندما يقع. فالمرء بالقسم الأول من القدرة . وقد ذهب الماتريدي مثل أبي حنيفة إلى أن القدرة الواحدة تصلح للأمرين، أي الطاعة والمعصية جميعا.
The principle according to him regarding that which is called 'power' (qudra) by which the act is realized is that it is of two types: one is that which prepares the ground for it by soundness of causes and correctness of instruments; and the second is a meaning that cannot be defined by anything except that it is only for the act, not possible to exist in any state except that the act occurs with it when it occurs. So man possesses the first type of power. And al-Maturidi, like Abu Hanifa, held that one single power suffices for both matters, i.e., obedience and disobedience together.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of power (qudra) according to Maturidi. The first type is the preparatory power that ensures sound causes and instruments. The second is the actual power that is concomitant with the act and cannot be defined otherwise. Maturidi agrees with Abu Hanifa that one power can serve for both obedience and disobedience, meaning a single capacity is sufficient for opposite actions.

Key Terms
قدرة
سبب
آلة
طاعة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different acts require different powers.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that a single power suffices for opposite acts, such as obedience and disobedience.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many types of qudra (power) are there?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
One power (qudra) can produce both obedience and disobedience, according to Maturidi and Abu Hanifa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been given a single capacity that can be used for either obedience or disobedience. Think of the last time you used that capacity for sin. Did you realize that the very same power could have been used for an act of worship? What does that reveal about your heart's orientation? Will you consciously redirect that power to obedience today?
?
The first type of power prepares the ground through sound causes and instruments. Consider your daily routines: are you intentionally setting up your environment and habits to facilitate obedience, or do you leave things to chance? Identify one specific 'cause' or 'instrument' you can adjust right now to make obedience easier and disobedience harder.
?
If the one power is neutral — equally capable of producing opposite actions — then the responsibility for which action emerges falls squarely on you. When you stand before Allah, will you have used His gift of ability for what pleases Him or for what angers Him? What will you change to ensure your single power is spent in obedience?

The Nature of Human Action and Its Limits
Maturidi enumerates the limits of human action and the role of human knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's view that human action is limited by surroundings, place, and limit, and that man's knowledge and power have a specified influence in its realization.

Core Idea
Human action is bounded by capacity; man's knowledge and power operate within defined limits.

Maturidi begins by describing the characteristics of human action. He emphasizes that while an action has many natural and psychological properties, the agent's knowledge and power are not absolute but limited. This sets the stage for understanding how human agency relates to divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's compressed style.
2
Identify the limitations
The passage states that action is from the capacity of surroundings, place, and limit. Find these and understand what they imply.
3
Understand the role of human knowledge and power
Maturidi acknowledges that man has knowledge of some properties and is a factor, but this influence is limited. Reflect on why this is important.
This connects to the idea of human agency without overstepping divine power.
4
Reflect on the status of true agent
Despite the limits, man attains the status of a true agent. Consider how Maturidi balances human responsibility with divine sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك، فإن الفعل من قدر المحيط والمكان والحد الذي لو أراد أن يعود إليه لا يمكن للإنسان تلاقيه؛ فالشيئ الذي في يده عبارة عن التحرك أو السكون نحو المنهي أو المأمور به . فيفهم مما تقدم أن الماتريدي يرى بأن فعلاً ما قد فعله الإنسان فله كثير من خاصياته الطبيعية والفيزيولوجية والنفسية، وأن فاعله مهما كان عنده علم بالبعض منها وأنه عامل في تحققها، فهو رغم ذلك يقبل بأن علم الإنسان وقدرته لهما نفوذ محدد أثناء تحقق هذا الفعل بالواقع. ومع هذا الفعل المحدود يأتى بصاحبه إلى مكانة الفاعل بمعناه الحقيقي. وقد قدم الماتريدي هناك مثالاً للفعل الواحد الذي يكون في الحقيقة لاثنين، فهو يوجب الشركة، نحو مد اثنين شيئًا ينقطع، وإزالة اثنين شيئًا عن مكان؛ فيُرى في الوهلة الأولى أن فعلهما واحد، والحقيقة هما يصيران بهذا الفعل شريكين لا في الفعل نفسه بل في أنه مفعولهما في الحقيقة، أي في الانقطاع والإزالة. ويعني ذلك أن فعل كل واحد في معناه الحقيقي منسوب إلى فاعله؛ فلا يجوز أن يملك أحد تقوية آخَر على فعله، ولا يقدر أن يخلق فعل الآخر. وكذلك لا أحد يقدر أن يفعل فعلاً في غير حيزه وغير حال في نفسه . ومن الجدير بالإشارة إلى أن كل واحد يتمتع بمقدرة روحية تامة ويعلم في نفسه أنه مختار لما يفعله من الأفعال، فبالتالي وجود ذلك ووجود علمه وخبره بأنه أعلى الخاصيات التي يتميز بها كإنسان هو الدليل الآخر الذي استخدمه الماتريدي في هذا المجال
That, for the act is from the capacity of the surroundings, place, and limit which, if he wanted to return to it, man cannot attain it; so the thing in his hand is an expression of movement or stillness towards the prohibited or commanded. So it is understood from the foregoing that al-Maturidi holds that an act done by man has many of its natural, physiological, and psychological properties, and that its agent, however much he may have knowledge of some of them and is a factor in their realization, nevertheless accepts that man's knowledge and capability have a limited influence during the realization of this act in reality. And with this limited act, he brings its doer to the status of a true agent. And al-Maturidi there gave an example of one act that in reality belongs to two, necessitating partnership, such as two pulling something until it breaks, and two removing something from a place; so at first glance it appears that their act is one, but in reality they become partners by this act not in the act itself but in that it is their effect in reality, i.e., in the breaking and removal. And this means that the act of each one in its true sense is attributed to its doer; so no one may empower another over his act, nor can he create the act of another. Likewise, no one can perform an act outside his own space and state of himself. And it is worth noting that each one possesses a complete spiritual capacity and knows in himself that he is free in choosing what he does of actions, so consequently the existence of that and the existence of his knowledge and his awareness that it is the highest of the properties by which he is distinguished as a human is the other proof that al-Maturidi used in this domain.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage explains Maturidi's view on human action and its relationship to divine action. It begins by describing the nature of human action as limited by surroundings and capacity, then clarifies that man's knowledge and power have a specified influence. The key example is that of two people pulling or removing something: their action appears single but is truly shared in the effect, not in the act itself. Each act is attributed to its true agent, and no one can empower another over his act or create another's act. The passage also notes that each person has a spiritual capacity and knows his own freedom, which serves as evidence for human agency.

Key Terms
فعل
فاعل
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, human action is fundamentally limited by which of the following?
A) Divine will
B) Surroundings, place, and limit
C) Human free will
D) Social norms

Key Takeaway
Human action is inherently limited; man's knowledge and power operate within these bounds, yet he is still considered a true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider any action you performed today, how aware were you of the real limits of your capacity and place? Does acknowledging these limits humble you or frustrate you? Will you now consciously recognize the boundaries of your own agency and still take responsibility for your actions?
?
The passage says man's knowledge and power have limited influence. Is there an area of your life where you have been acting as if you have unlimited control? What would it change if you truly accepted your limited role and submitted to the true Agent of all actions?
?
Despite the limits, you are still considered a true agent and will be held accountable. How does this balance of limited influence and full responsibility affect your sense of moral urgency? Are you more likely to make excuses or to strive within your bounds?

Partnership of Two Agents in One Effect
Maturidi exemplifies partnership in effect and asserts each agent's act is his own.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's example of two agents sharing an effect and his conclusion that each act is attributed solely to its doer.

Core Idea
When two agents share an effect, they are partners in the effect, not in the act itself.

Maturidi presents a concrete example to illustrate how two agents can be involved in what appears to be a single act. He clarifies that the partnership lies in the produced effect, not in the act itself. This leads to the principle that each agent's act is uniquely attributed to him.

Guided Path
1
Read the example
Read Maturidi's example of two people pulling something until it breaks, and two removing something from a place. Understand the scenario.
This is a physical example to illustrate a metaphysical point.
2
Distinguish act and effect
Maturidi says that at first glance their act appears one, but in reality they are partners in the effect (the breaking or removal), not in the act itself. Grasp this distinction.
3
Grasp the conclusion
From this, Maturidi concludes that each act in its true sense is attributed to its doer, and no one can empower another over his act or create another's act.
This upholds individual responsibility.
4
Note the further evidence
Maturidi adds that each person has a complete spiritual capacity and knows his own freedom, using this as additional proof for human agency.
This reinforces the idea that humans are true agents despite limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك، فإن الفعل من قدر المحيط والمكان والحد الذي لو أراد أن يعود إليه لا يمكن للإنسان تلاقيه؛ فالشيئ الذي في يده عبارة عن التحرك أو السكون نحو المنهي أو المأمور به . فيفهم مما تقدم أن الماتريدي يرى بأن فعلاً ما قد فعله الإنسان فله كثير من خاصياته الطبيعية والفيزيولوجية والنفسية، وأن فاعله مهما كان عنده علم بالبعض منها وأنه عامل في تحققها، فهو رغم ذلك يقبل بأن علم الإنسان وقدرته لهما نفوذ محدد أثناء تحقق هذا الفعل بالواقع. ومع هذا الفعل المحدود يأتى بصاحبه إلى مكانة الفاعل بمعناه الحقيقي. وقد قدم الماتريدي هناك مثالاً للفعل الواحد الذي يكون في الحقيقة لاثنين، فهو يوجب الشركة، نحو مد اثنين شيئًا ينقطع، وإزالة اثنين شيئًا عن مكان؛ فيُرى في الوهلة الأولى أن فعلهما واحد، والحقيقة هما يصيران بهذا الفعل شريكين لا في الفعل نفسه بل في أنه مفعولهما في الحقيقة، أي في الانقطاع والإزالة. ويعني ذلك أن فعل كل واحد في معناه الحقيقي منسوب إلى فاعله؛ فلا يجوز أن يملك أحد تقوية آخَر على فعله، ولا يقدر أن يخلق فعل الآخر. وكذلك لا أحد يقدر أن يفعل فعلاً في غير حيزه وغير حال في نفسه . ومن الجدير بالإشارة إلى أن كل واحد يتمتع بمقدرة روحية تامة ويعلم في نفسه أنه مختار لما يفعله من الأفعال، فبالتالي وجود ذلك ووجود علمه وخبره بأنه أعلى الخاصيات التي يتميز بها كإنسان هو الدليل الآخر الذي استخدمه الماتريدي في هذا المجال
That, for the act is from the capacity of the surroundings, place, and limit which, if he wanted to return to it, man cannot attain it; so the thing in his hand is an expression of movement or stillness towards the prohibited or commanded. So it is understood from the foregoing that al-Maturidi holds that an act done by man has many of its natural, physiological, and psychological properties, and that its agent, however much he may have knowledge of some of them and is a factor in their realization, nevertheless accepts that man's knowledge and capability have a limited influence during the realization of this act in reality. And with this limited act, he brings its doer to the status of a true agent. And al-Maturidi there gave an example of one act that in reality belongs to two, necessitating partnership, such as two pulling something until it breaks, and two removing something from a place; so at first glance it appears that their act is one, but in reality they become partners by this act not in the act itself but in that it is their effect in reality, i.e., in the breaking and removal. And this means that the act of each one in its true sense is attributed to its doer; so no one may empower another over his act, nor can he create the act of another. Likewise, no one can perform an act outside his own space and state of himself. And it is worth noting that each one possesses a complete spiritual capacity and knows in himself that he is free in choosing what he does of actions, so consequently the existence of that and the existence of his knowledge and his awareness that it is the highest of the properties by which he is distinguished as a human is the other proof that al-Maturidi used in this domain.

Commentary
This passage explains Maturidi's view on human action and its relationship to divine action. It begins by describing the nature of human action as limited by surroundings and capacity, then clarifies that man's knowledge and power have a specified influence. The key example is that of two people pulling or removing something: their action appears single but is truly shared in the effect, not in the act itself. Misconception & Correction

Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the partnership is in the effect (e.g., breaking or removal), not in the act itself.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's example, in what sense do two agents become partners in a single act?
A) In the act itself
B) In the effect of the act, such as breaking or removing
C) In the intention
D) In the physical movement

Key Takeaway
Two agents can share an effect, but each individual act remains attributed to its own agent; no one can create another's act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent situation where you worked with someone and the outcome was shared—whether success or failure. According to this lesson, your act is entirely yours. What part of that outcome is solely your doing? Can you own it without deflecting or inflating your role? This week, in one specific interaction, consciously identify and take responsibility for your own act, leaving the other's act to them. How does that feel?
?
This lesson demonstrates that no one can empower another over his act or create another's act. Is there someone in your life whose choices you try to control or take credit for? Or are you allowing someone else to take responsibility for your actions? Name one relationship where you need to clarify the boundaries of agency. What one step can you take today to respect their act as theirs and yours as yours?
?
The passage ends by pointing to your inner awareness: you know you are free in what you choose to do. Pause now and recall a moment today when you made a choice—small or large. Did you truly feel it was your choice, or did you mentally attribute it to habit, pressure, or circumstances? If you fully embraced that it was your act, how might you have done it differently? Commit to one deliberate choice tomorrow, and before acting, say to yourself: 'This act is mine. I own it.'

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Impossibility of Infinite Regress
Maturidi argues that requiring a prior thing for existence leads to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why requiring a prior condition for every thing leads to an infinite regress, and how this proves the necessity of a beginning (the First Cause).

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible; a beginning must exist.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful logical argument: if every thing requires something prior to it, then nothing can ever come into existence. He uses simple analogies to show the absurdity of infinite regress and to establish that creation must have a beginning. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Note the terms 'aghyar' (other things) and 'baqa'' (remaining).
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage presents a conditional: if every thing requires a prior different thing, then the existence of all such things is invalid. Recognize this as a proof against infinite regress.
3
Analyze the analogies
Maturidi gives two analogies: eating with a precondition and the doubling of reckoning. See how each illustrates the impossibility of an infinite chain without a starting point.
The first analogy shows perpetual non-eating; the second shows that without a beginning, nothing can exist.
4
Draw the conclusion
The argument concludes that temporal existence must have a beginning. Understand how this refutes the notion of an infinite regress of causes.
This is a foundational argument for the existence of a First Cause in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الشيء إذا لم يكن إلا بِغَيْرِ يتقدمه، وذلك شرط كل الأغيار، فيبطل كون الجميع، ولا كذلك أمر البقاء. ألا ترى أن من قال لآخر : لا تأكل شيئًا حتى تأكل غيره - وكذا كل غير فيه ذلك الشرط - فيبقى أبداً غير آكل. ولو قال : كلما أكلت لقمة فكل أخرى فهو يبقى أبدا في الأكل، فمثله الأول. وعلى ذلك أمر تضاعف الحساب، إنه إذا لم يُجعل له ابتداء منه يُبدأ لا يجوز وجود شيء منه بَنّة، وإذا حصل البداية يجوز أن يبقى فيه فيما يزيد، ثم يزيد دائما. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, if a thing is only [existing] by something prior to it, and that is the condition of all other things (al-aghyar), then the existence of all [such things] is invalid, and the matter of remaining (al-baqa') is not like that. Do you not see that if someone says to another: 'Do not eat anything until you eat something else' - and likewise every other thing has that condition - then he remains forever not eating. And if he says: 'Whenever you eat a morsel, eat another' - then he remains forever eating. The first is similar. And likewise is the matter of the doubling of reckoning: if no beginning is made from which one starts, no part of it can exist at all; and if a beginning occurs, it is possible to continue in it increasing, then increase forever. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage uses an analogy to show that if every thing requires a prior different thing for its existence, then no thing can ever come into existence, because each prior thing itself would require another prior thing ad infinitum. This is contrasted with the concept of remaining (baqa'), which does not have this problem. The analogy of eating illustrates: a command to eat one thing after another leads to perpetual eating with a beginning, but a command that each thing requires a prior different thing leads to perpetual non-eating. The same applies to the multiplication of numbers: without a starting point, nothing can exist, but with a start, continuation is possible. Thus, temporal existence must have a beginning.

Key Terms
أغيار
بقاء
ابتداء
تضاعف الحساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies causality altogether.
Correction: The argument does not deny causality; it proves that a series of prior conditions cannot be infinite and must have a first cause.

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of eating illustrate?
A) That remaining requires no prior thing
B) That an infinite regress is impossible
C) That eating is always possible
D) That all things are eternal

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress proves that creation must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Just as the man in Maturidi's analogy could never eat because each bite required a prior bite, what 'prior conditions' are you waiting for before you take a step of faith—praying, repenting, starting a good deed? Are you trapped in an infinite regress of excuses? How does the impossibility of infinite regress challenge you to act now, starting with a sincere first step?
?
Maturidi proves that without a beginning, nothing can exist. Your own existence, your faith, your good deeds—do you truly recognize that they depend entirely on God as the First Cause, or do you live as if your life is a self-perpetuating cycle? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that every good thing must begin with Him?
?
The passage contrasts the impossibility of an infinite prior series with the possibility of ongoing increase after a beginning. In what area of your spiritual life are you trying to sustain growth—knowledge, worship, character—without properly establishing a firm foundation? Are you avoiding the difficult starting point? What is one concrete beginning you will make today?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
The Impossibility of an Infinite Regress of Accidents
Maturidi argues: without a first accident, no accident can exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that an infinite regress of accidents without a beginning is impossible, and therefore anything subject to accidents must have a beginning.

Core Idea
Without a first accident, no accident can exist.

Maturidi presents a concise but powerful argument about the nature of accidents and bodies. He asks us to imagine a scenario without bodies, but with a series of accidents. If that series has no first accident, then no accident can ever exist — the chain cannot start. This reasoning leads to the conclusion that anything that is never free from accidents must itself have a beginning. This is a cornerstone of the kalam cosmological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once without translating. Notice the conditional structure: 'لو توهمنا...'. Let the thought experiment sink in.
The Arabic uses a hypothetical scenario starting with 'لو توهمنا'.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage contrasts two scenarios: one without a first accident (result: nothing can exist) and one with a beginning (result: infinite continuation possible).
The key phrase is 'لم يجز وجود شيء منه' vs 'ويجوز الوجود أبداً'.
3
Apply to bodies
The passage concludes by saying 'so likewise is that which is never free from accidents'. Understand that this refers to physical bodies.
Bodies are never free from accidents, so they must have a beginning.
4
Reflect on the implication
If bodies require a beginning, then the world is temporal and requires a Creator. This is a key proof for the existence of God in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا لو توهمنا أن لا جسم وأنه يجوز وجود عَرَض قبل عَرَض لم يجز وجود شيء منه، إذ لم يُجعل له أولية وابتداء؛ ويجوز الوجود أبداً بلا نهاية له، فمثله ما لا يخلو عن الأعراض. والله الموفق.
And also, if we suppose that there is no body and that it is possible for an accident to exist before an accident, then no part of it can exist, since no priority and beginning are assigned to it; and existence forever without end is possible. So likewise is that which is never free from accidents. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents a thought experiment: if we imagine a scenario without any body, and we allow one accident to exist before another in a series, but without assigning a first accident, then no accident can ever exist because the series would have no starting point. However, if a beginning is allowed, then an infinite continuation is possible. The same logic applies to bodies that are never free from accidents: if the series of accidents has no beginning, the body itself could not exist. This is a standard kalam argument for the temporality of the world: since bodies are always accompanied by accidents, and accidents have a beginning, bodies too must have a beginning.

Key Terms
عرض
جسم
أولية
ابتداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that accidents exist or that they can be infinite.
Correction: The argument does not deny that accidents exist; it shows that an infinite regress of accidents without a beginning is impossible. It allows infinite continuation once a beginning is established.

Checkpoint
What does this argument conclude about something that is never free from accidents?
A) It must be eternal
B) It must have a beginning
C) It can exist without accidents
D) It is impossible

Key Takeaway
Anything that is never free from accidents must have a beginning, because an infinite regress of accidents without a first cause is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are never free from change: every moment, your thoughts, emotions, and body experience new accidents. If this proves you must have a beginning, how does that change the way you live today, knowing your existence is not self-sufficient?
?
What temporary 'accident' in your life—your job, reputation, health—are you clinging to as if it will last forever? If nothing impermanent can exist without a First Cause, what does that say about your ultimate reliance?
?
This argument shows that every chain of events requires a starting point. In your own life, what is the first thing that gives meaning to all your daily actions? If it is not God, will it not ultimately lead to an infinite regress of empty purposes?

Motion and Composition Prove a Beginning
Maturidi argues that every motion and composition points to an endpoint of the past, which implies a starting point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the observation of motion and composition to argue that the physical world must have a beginning.

Core Idea
Every end implies a start — so motion proves a finite past.

This lesson presents a concise proof from Imam Maturidi for the temporal beginning of the world. He observes that any motion or composition we see is the end of a series of prior motions or compositions. But an end can only exist if the series had a beginning. This simple yet powerful argument is a cornerstone of kalam cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi states his premise and draws the conclusion.
The argument is only two sentences but dense.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the terms 'motion' (حركة) and 'composition' (اجتماع) as examples of events that have an endpoint. Reflect on what 'end of what has passed' means.
This series is not individual motions but the whole category.
3
Understand the logical implication
Ask: Why does an end require a beginning? Maturidi says it is impossible (محال) for an end to exist without a beginning. Understand that this is a rational necessity.
The commentary explains the implication.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
This argument shows that the physical world is not eternal. It must have been brought into existence. This leads to the need for a Creator.
This is a classic kalam proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن كل حركة أو اجتماع نشير إليه هو نهاية ما مضى من ذلك النوع، فمحال وجود نهاية الماضي بلا ابتداء له. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, every motion or composition that we point to is the end of what has passed of that type, so it is impossible for an end of the past to exist without a beginning for it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This is a concise argument: any specific motion or composition we observe is the endpoint of a series of previous motions or compositions. But an endpoint can only exist if the series had a beginning; otherwise, there would be no endpoint. Therefore, motion and composition must have a starting point, implying that the physical world is temporally finite. This is a classic kalam proof for the world's having a beginning.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about each individual motion being finite.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is about the entire series of motions or compositions. He says 'what has passed of that type' meaning all prior instances of that kind. The endpoint is the most recent one, and the series as a whole must have a beginning.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for an end of the past to exist without a beginning?
A) Because every end must have a start
B) Because the past is infinite
C) Because motion is eternal
D) Because composition is eternal

Key Takeaway
Every observed motion or composition is an endpoint of a series that must have had a beginning, proving the world is not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every motion you have ever made is an end of a past series that must have had a beginning, what does that mean for your own most significant actions? How does this realization make you accountable to the One who started it all?
?
This lesson ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' — 'And there is no power except with God.' When you consider your own daily efforts and struggles, do you truly rely on God's power, or do you secretly believe your own motion and striving are self-sufficient?
?
Imagine standing at the endpoint of your life's motions — what beginning are you pointing back to? If the world itself had a beginning, what does that demand of you regarding your relationship with the Creator?

Body's Remaining and Successive Accidents
Maturidi explains bodily persistence through successive accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a body can be described as 'remaining' through successive accidents, and why this does not imply the body's pre-eternity.

Core Idea
A body's 'remaining' is a series of renewed accidents, not a single enduring quality.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a subtle point about the nature of bodily persistence. How can we say a body 'remains' if the accident of remaining itself does not endure? Maturidi explains that each moment a new accident of remaining occurs, preserving the body. This understanding also prevents us from mistakenly using the occurrence of accidents to argue that the body is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let Maturidi's argument come through naturally.
Notice the direct style of reasoning.
2
Understand successive remaining
Maturidi says a body can be described as remaining with a single remaining that does not itself endure. This implies that remaining is renewed continuously.
Think of it like a series of snapshots of existence.
3
Refute pre-eternity claim
Maturidi argues that the temporality (huduth) of remaining in the body is the cause of preservation, not a sign that the body is pre-eternal. An accident cannot cause the body's pre-eternity.
This guards against a specific misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن قد يوصف جسم بالبقاء مع بقاء واحد وإن كان ذلك البقاء لا يبقى، ولا يوصف جسم بالعدم مع حدث واحد لا يخلوا عنه، فكذلك ما كثر منه. على أن حدوث البقاء في الجسم هو سبب إبقائه، ويدوم بقاؤه على دوام تتابع البقاء فيه. ولا يجوز أن يكون سبب قدم الجسم حدوث عرض فيه فصار هو عديله وقرينه؛ فلذلك لا يتقدمه. والله الموفق.
And also, a body may be described as remaining with a single remaining, even if that remaining does not endure, and a body is not described as non-existence with a single temporal origination that it never lacks. So likewise with what is more than that. Indeed, the temporality of remaining in the body is the cause of its preservation, and its remaining continues with the continuity of the succession of remaining in it. And it is not permissible that the cause of the body's pre-eternity be the temporality of an accident occurring in it, so that it becomes its equal and companion; therefore, it is not preceded by it. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage clarifies that a body can be said to have 'remaining' (baqa') even if each individual instance of remaining does not itself persist, because successive new instances of remaining keep the body in existence. The temporal origination (huduth) of the accident of remaining in the body is what preserves the body, not a sign that the body is pre-eternal. One cannot argue that because an accident occurs in the body, the body must be eternal, because the accident is temporally originated and thus cannot be the cause of the body's pre-eternity. The accident and the body are not equals; the accident is subsequent.

Key Terms
بقاء
حدث
جسم
تتابع
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'remaining' of a body is a single enduring quality that persists unchanged.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that remaining is a series of renewed accidents; each instance of remaining does not itself endure but is renewed successively.

Checkpoint
What is the cause of a body's preservation according to this passage?
A) The body's own pre-eternity
B) The temporality of remaining occurring in it
C) The absence of accidents
D) The eternality of accidents

Key Takeaway
A body persists through successive accidents of remaining, not through any inherent pre-eternality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though your existence is renewed by Allah every moment, or do you behave as if you are self-sustaining? What would change if you deeply believed that your next breath is a new creation?
?
If your 'remaining' is a series of accidents that Allah recreates each instant, why do you fear death as though your existence is a fixed thing you possess? How does this understanding affect your attachment to this world?
?
When you sin, do you consider that Allah is sustaining your existence even as you disobey Him? How should that awareness transform your repentance?

Pre-Islamic Theories of Eternal Substrate
Maturidi enumerates five false theories of creation from an eternal substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five pre-Islamic theories of creation from an eternal substrate that Maturidi refutes.

Core Idea
Theories of an eternal substrate fail because they cannot avoid absurdity.

Imam al-Maturidi begins his refutation by listing the erroneous views held by various groups regarding the origin of the world. These theories share the assumption that the world came from some pre-existing eternal matter or emanation, rather than being created ex nihilo. By enumerating these views, Maturidi sets the stage for his logical critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of views
Read the Arabic passage listing views 2–6. Each view proposes a different eternal substrate or emanation process.
Notice that Maturidi includes brief comments on some views, especially regarding causality.
2
Identify each theory's claim
List each view and its key claim: eternal clay (ṭīna), hyle (hayūlā), causation by Creator, etc.
View 2 posits an infinite chain; view 3 eternal clay; view 4 accidents; view 5 the Creator; view 6 hyle.
3
Understand why they proposed these theories
These theories were attempts to avoid creation from nothing, which seemed inconceivable to them.
Maturidi notes that they 'could not bear affirming otherwise'.

البقاء بما لا يبقى . ۲ - ومنهم من يقول بكون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بمنشيء حكيم، وجعلوه علة كون العالم، ومحال كون العلة ولا معلول. مع ما لا يخلو من [أن] لا يوصف بالقدرة والجود في القدم وذلك آية العجز والحاجة، أو يوصف فيجب المقدور عليه وإفاضة الجود على كل شيء؛ وما ذُكِرَ من التوهم لهم أيضاً . - ومنهم من يقول بقدم الطينة، وهي الأصل، وحدَثِ الصَّنْعة. { قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } فقوله بقدم الطينة لما ذكرنا من رفع كون شيء لا عن شيء. ثم كان كل شيء حَدَث عن شيء حدث عند انقلاب الأول وهلاكه ، نحو ما يحدث من النطفة والبيضة . ٤ ـ ومنهم من جعل حدثه بعوارض حلّت بالطينة فانقلبت إلى ما عليه الطبائع من الاعتدال والاختلاف. ه - ومنهم من جعل حدثه بالباري. ٦ - ومنهم من قال بالأصل وسماه هَيُولَى . قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : } فجملة ما ذهب إليه هؤلاء دفع ما لا يتصور في الوهم ولا يتمثل في النفس، إذ كذلك وجد ولم تحتمل قلوبهم إيجاب خلافه. فيقال: أيتصوّر في أوهامكم دفع ما لا يتمثل في النفس ؟ فان قال : نعم ، كَابَرَ ، لمشاركتنا إياه في ذي الصور، وليس يتصوّر دفعه هذا في أوهامنا . وإن قال : لا ، بل تقديره ؛ فيقال له : متى يتصوّر في الوهم قدم الشيء أو بقاؤه بعد التفرق وأن يصير بحيث لا يأخذه البصر ؟ وقد يقول بذلك كله . ومع ذلك في الأنْفُس ما لا يتمثل من السمع والبصر وجَرْي قوى جوهر واحد من الطعام [١٥ظ] وتولّد قوة الجواهر المختلفة به كالسمع والبصر والفهم واليد والرجل وغيرها، فما ينكر مثله في تلك الجملة بالأدلة. ثم يقال له: لا يعدو كون الشيء من الشيء من أن يكون مستجنّاً فيه فظهر . وذلك محال : جميع البشر أن يكون الإنسان بكليته، والشجر بكليته مع ما يثمر يكون في ذلك الأصل؛ أو بجوهرهم يكونون في أصل الماء الذي كان في صلب، فيسع في الشيء الواحد ما لا يحصى من الأضعاف. وذلك مما لا يُحتمل تمثله في نفس صحيحة ولا يصبر عليه عقل سليم. وذلك يبطل قوله كون الشيء من الشيء، لأنه بكليته لم يكن من النطفة. وليس له أن يدعى كونه في الأغذية؛ لأنه يبلغ وقتا في العِظم لا يزداد ألبتة، وتلك الأغذية كلها موجودة أو فيها زيادة بالجوهر. وكم من جوهر يُسمن ، وآخر يأكل ذلك عُمْرَه فلا يظهر؛ وترى التوت وورقه يأكله نَعَم فيخرج من كلّ غير الذي يخرج من غيره، وكذلك الثمر وغيره. فهذا يبين أن ذلك ليس بعمل الأغذية. على أن الأغذية هُنَّ مَوَاتٌ لا يُحتمل أن تصير كذلك إلا بتدبير مدبر عليم، لا أن استفادَ ذلك المعنى من غيره بلا تدبير. وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالذي قلنا. أو أنْ كان حَدَث شيء منه أو بعضه - لا أن كان [مستجنّا] في شيء مما ذكر ـ فيجب القول بحدث العالم بما لزم في بعضه. ثم يقال لهم : إذ كل مشاهد ذو نهاية ، وجعلتموه دليل العالم، لم لا كان الكل كذلك؟ وإلا
Perpetuity by that which does not remain. 2 - And among them is one who says that something comes from something else ad infinitum by a wise originator, and they made it the cause of the existence of the world, and it is impossible for the cause to exist without an effect. Along with that, it cannot be free from [that] He is not described with power and generosity in pre-eternity, and that is a sign of incapacity and need, or He is described, then the enabled thing and the pouring forth of generosity upon everything become necessary; and what was mentioned of illusion for them as well. - And among them is one who says that the clay (al-ṭīna) is eternal, and it is the origin, and the craft is temporal. {Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said:} So his statement of the eternity of the clay is due to what we mentioned of removing the existence of something from nothing. Then everything that came to be from something came to be at the transformation and destruction of the first, like what comes to be from the sperm and the egg. 4 - And among them is one who made its occurrence by accidents that befell the clay, and it transformed into what the natures are upon of moderation and difference. 5 - And among them is one who made its occurrence by the Creator (al-Bārī). 6 - And among them is one who said with the origin and called it hyle (hayūlā). The Sheikh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The sum of what these went to is the repudiation of what is not conceivable in imagination nor represented in the mind, thus they found it (the world) and their hearts could not bear affirming otherwise. So it is said: Is it conceivable in your imaginations to repudiate what is not represented in the mind? If he says: Yes, he is obstinate, because of our sharing with him in that which has forms, and this repudiation is not conceivable in our imaginations. And if he says: No, but rather (it is a matter of) estimation; so it is said to him: When is it conceivable in the imagination the pre-existence of a thing or its permanence after separation and its becoming such that vision does not perceive it? And he may say all of that. Yet in the minds there is what is not represented, like hearing and sight and the flow of the powers of one substance from food [15ẓ] and the generation of the power of different substances through it, like hearing, sight, understanding, hand, foot, and others, so what denies its like in that totality through proofs? Then it is said to him: The existence of something from something does not go beyond being hidden within it and then appearing. And that is impossible: that the human being in his entirety, and the tree in its entirety with what it produces, should be in that origin; or that they should be in their substance in the origin of water that was in the backbone, so that in one thing there would be room for countless multiples. That is something whose representation cannot be entertained in a sound mind, nor can a sound intellect bear it. And that invalidates his statement of the existence of something from something, because it (the human) in its entirety did not come from the sperm. And he cannot claim its existence in foods; because it reaches a time in size when it does not increase at all, and all those foods are present or there is increase in substance. How many a substance fattens, and another eats it all its life and does not show; and you see the mulberry and its leaf which cattle eat, and from each comes out other than what comes out from others, and likewise the fruit and other things. This shows that this is not the work of foods. Moreover, foods are lifeless; it is not conceivable that they become such except by the administration of a wise administrator, not that they acquired that meaning from something else without administration. In that is the necessity of saying what we said. Or if something came to be from it or a part of it - not that it was hidden in something of what was mentioned - then the statement of the temporality of the world is necessitated by what is necessary in part of it. Then it is said to them: Since every observed thing has an end, and you made it a proof of the world, why is not the whole likewise? Otherwise...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative refutation by Imam al-Maturidi of several pre-Islamic theories that posited an eternal substrate or emanation for the created world. The passage first enumerates six erroneous views (enumerated as 2 through 6, with 1 presumably preceding on an earlier page). View 2 holds that things come from other things ad infinitum, driven by a wise originator who is the cause of the world, implying a cause without an effect. Maturidi points out that either that originator is not described with power and generosity in pre-eternity (a sign of need) or He is, in which case the effect and bestowal of goodness must be eternal—a self-contradiction. View 3 posits an eternal clay (al-ṭīna) as the original substance, with only the subsequent craft being temporal. Maturidi comments that this view arose from avoiding creation ex nihilo, but then all temporal things would arise from something else only through the transformation of the first substance, analogous to sperm and egg. View 4 attributes the world's origin to accidents that befell the eternal clay, causing it to transform into the various natures. View 5 attributes the origin directly to the Creator (al-Bārī) but likely still implies some form of emanation. View 6 calls the original substance 'hyle' (hayūlā). After listing these views, Maturidi summarizes their common core: they all attempt to repudiate what is not conceivable in imagination or representable in the mind—that is, creation from nothing—because they found the world existing and could not bear to affirm its coming from nothing. Maturidi then launches a dialectical refutation. He asks: 'Is it conceivable in your imaginations to repudiate what is not represented in the mind?' If they say yes, they are obstinate, because we all share the same faculty of imagination regarding things that have forms, and this repudiation of the non-imaginable is not itself imaginable. If they say no (i.e., it is merely a matter of estimation), then he asks: when is it conceivable in the imagination the pre-existence of a thing or its permanence after separation and its becoming imperceptible? They might accept such possibilities. Maturidi then points out that there are many things in the mind that are not representable to the senses, like hearing, sight, the flow of powers from one substance, and the generation of different powers from food. Therefore, one cannot deny by mere imagination the possibility of the world coming from nothing. He then argues more directly: the concept of 'something from something' must mean that the second thing was hidden in the first and then appeared. But this is impossible in many cases: a whole human being or tree cannot be hidden in a single sperm or seed. Even if one says it is hidden in substance, the substance cannot contain countless individuals. Furthermore, the human body in its entirety does not come from the sperm alone—it increases from food. But this increase cannot be attributed to the food itself, because food is lifeless and only produces growth through a wise administrator (tadbīr mudabbir ʿalīm). Thus, the argument of 'something from something' breaks down and actually proves the need for a Creator. Finally, Maturidi uses a reductio: if one accepts that every observed thing has a finite end (being temporal), and they use that as a proof for the world's temporality, then why is not the whole world also temporal? The argument concludes with the necessity of affirming creation from nothing by a wise Creator.

Key Terms
الطينة
الهيولى
العلة
المعلول
الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think these ancient theories are plausible alternatives to creation ex nihilo.
Correction: Maturidi shows that each theory leads to logical contradictions or absurdities, demonstrating that only creation from nothing is rational.

Checkpoint
Which view posits that an eternal clay (al-ṭīna) is the original substance?
A) View 2
B) View 3
C) View 4
D) View 6

Key Takeaway
Maturidi identifies five flawed theories of creation, all of which assume an eternal substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that God created the world from nothing by His sheer command, would you live today as though your very existence depends on Him alone? What would change if you did?
?
When you consider the theory of eternal clay, do you feel a deep-seated need for something in creation to be self-sufficient? Where in your life are you trying to preserve that illusion?

Causality and Created Things
advanced

Maturidi's Refutation of Eternal Substrate Theories
Maturidi refutes the theories with logical arguments, focusing on the impossibility of 'something from something'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical argument that the concept of 'something from something' is impossible and leads to the necessity of a Creator.

Core Idea
Maturidi refutes the common notion that creation must be from something, showing it leads to absurdities.

After listing the false theories, Maturidi presents a powerful logical refutation. He first diagnoses the root of their error: they attempt to repudiate what cannot be imagined—creation from nothing. Then, through a series of questions and reductio ad absurdum, he demonstrates that the very idea of 'something coming from something' is incoherent and ultimately proves the need for a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the passage starting from 'The sum of what these went to...' to the end. Notice the dialectical style: question, response, counter-argument.
This passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Analyze the dialectical question
Maturidi asks if they can repudiate what is unimaginable. Consider the two possible answers and their refutations.
This reveals the weakness of relying on imagination.
3
Understand the argument against 'something from something'
Maturidi shows that if a thing comes from another, it must be hidden within it, but this is impossible for whole beings. This refutes the theory.
The example of a human from sperm is key.
4
Conclude with the necessity of a Creator
The argument ends by showing that only a wise administrator can explain the observable diversity and growth, not the substrate itself.
This reinforces the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo.

البقاء بما لا يبقى . ۲ - ومنهم من يقول بكون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بمنشيء حكيم، وجعلوه علة كون العالم، ومحال كون العلة ولا معلول. مع ما لا يخلو من [أن] لا يوصف بالقدرة والجود في القدم وذلك آية العجز والحاجة، أو يوصف فيجب المقدور عليه وإفاضة الجود على كل شيء؛ وما ذُكِرَ من التوهم لهم أيضاً . - ومنهم من يقول بقدم الطينة، وهي الأصل، وحدَثِ الصَّنْعة. { قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } فقوله بقدم الطينة لما ذكرنا من رفع كون شيء لا عن شيء. ثم كان كل شيء حَدَث عن شيء حدث عند انقلاب الأول وهلاكه ، نحو ما يحدث من النطفة والبيضة . ٤ ـ ومنهم من جعل حدثه بعوارض حلّت بالطينة فانقلبت إلى ما عليه الطبائع من الاعتدال والاختلاف. ه - ومنهم من جعل حدثه بالباري. ٦ - ومنهم من قال بالأصل وسماه هَيُولَى . قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : } فجملة ما ذهب إليه هؤلاء دفع ما لا يتصور في الوهم ولا يتمثل في النفس، إذ كذلك وجد ولم تحتمل قلوبهم إيجاب خلافه. فيقال: أيتصوّر في أوهامكم دفع ما لا يتمثل في النفس ؟ فان قال : نعم ، كَابَرَ ، لمشاركتنا إياه في ذي الصور، وليس يتصوّر دفعه هذا في أوهامنا . وإن قال : لا ، بل تقديره ؛ فيقال له : متى يتصوّر في الوهم قدم الشيء أو بقاؤه بعد التفرق وأن يصير بحيث لا يأخذه البصر ؟ وقد يقول بذلك كله . ومع ذلك في الأنْفُس ما لا يتمثل من السمع والبصر وجَرْي قوى جوهر واحد من الطعام [١٥ظ] وتولّد قوة الجواهر المختلفة به كالسمع والبصر والفهم واليد والرجل وغيرها، فما ينكر مثله في تلك الجملة بالأدلة. ثم يقال له: لا يعدو كون الشيء من الشيء من أن يكون مستجنّاً فيه فظهر . وذلك محال : جميع البشر أن يكون الإنسان بكليته، والشجر بكليته مع ما يثمر يكون في ذلك الأصل؛ أو بجوهرهم يكونون في أصل الماء الذي كان في صلب، فيسع في الشيء الواحد ما لا يحصى من الأضعاف. وذلك مما لا يُحتمل تمثله في نفس صحيحة ولا يصبر عليه عقل سليم. وذلك يبطل قوله كون الشيء من الشيء، لأنه بكليته لم يكن من النطفة. وليس له أن يدعى كونه في الأغذية؛ لأنه يبلغ وقتا في العِظم لا يزداد ألبتة، وتلك الأغذية كلها موجودة أو فيها زيادة بالجوهر. وكم من جوهر يُسمن ، وآخر يأكل ذلك عُمْرَه فلا يظهر؛ وترى التوت وورقه يأكله نَعَم فيخرج من كلّ غير الذي يخرج من غيره، وكذلك الثمر وغيره. فهذا يبين أن ذلك ليس بعمل الأغذية. على أن الأغذية هُنَّ مَوَاتٌ لا يُحتمل أن تصير كذلك إلا بتدبير مدبر عليم، لا أن استفادَ ذلك المعنى من غيره بلا تدبير. وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالذي قلنا. أو أنْ كان حَدَث شيء منه أو بعضه - لا أن كان [مستجنّا] في شيء مما ذكر ـ فيجب القول بحدث العالم بما لزم في بعضه. ثم يقال لهم : إذ كل مشاهد ذو نهاية ، وجعلتموه دليل العالم، لم لا كان الكل كذلك؟ وإلا
Perpetuity by that which does not remain. 2 - And among them is one who says that something comes from something else ad infinitum by a wise originator, and they made it the cause of the existence of the world, and it is impossible for the cause to exist without an effect. Along with that, it cannot be free from [that] He is not described with power and generosity in pre-eternity, and that is a sign of incapacity and need, or He is described, then the enabled thing and the pouring forth of generosity upon everything become necessary; and what was mentioned of illusion for them as well. - And among them is one who says that the clay (al-ṭīna) is eternal, and it is the origin, and the craft is temporal. {Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said:} So his statement of the eternity of the clay is due to what we mentioned of removing the existence of something from nothing. Then everything that came to be from something came to be at the transformation and destruction of the first, like what comes to be from the sperm and the egg. 4 - And among them is one who made its occurrence by accidents that befell the clay, and it transformed into what the natures are upon of moderation and difference. 5 - And among them is one who made its occurrence by the Creator (al-Bārī). 6 - And among them is one who said with the origin and called it hyle (hayūlā). The Sheikh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The sum of what these went to is the repudiation of what is not conceivable in imagination nor represented in the mind, thus they found it (the world) and their hearts could not bear affirming otherwise. So it is said: Is it conceivable in your imaginations to repudiate what is not represented in the mind? If he says: Yes, he is obstinate, because of our sharing with him in that which has forms, and this repudiation is not conceivable in our imaginations. And if he says: No, but rather (it is a matter of) estimation; so it is said to him: When is it conceivable in the imagination the pre-existence of a thing or its permanence after separation and its becoming such that vision does not perceive it? And he may say all of that. Yet in the minds there is what is not represented, like hearing and sight and the flow of the powers of one substance from food [15ẓ] and the generation of the power of different substances through it, like hearing, sight, understanding, hand, foot, and others, so what denies its like in that totality through proofs? Then it is said to him: The existence of something from something does not go beyond being hidden within it and then appearing. And that is impossible: that the human being in his entirety, and the tree in its entirety with what it produces, should be in that origin; or that they should be in their substance in the origin of water that was in the backbone, so that in one thing there would be room for countless multiples. That is something whose representation cannot be entertained in a sound mind, nor can a sound intellect bear it. And that invalidates his statement of the existence of something from something, because it (the human) in its entirety did not come from the sperm. And he cannot claim its existence in foods; because it reaches a time in size when it does not increase at all, and all those foods are present or there is increase in substance. How many a substance fattens, and another eats it all its life and does not show; and you see the mulberry and its leaf which cattle eat, and from each comes out other than what comes out from others, and likewise the fruit and other things. This shows that this is not the work of foods. Moreover, foods are lifeless; it is not conceivable that they become such except by the administration of a wise administrator, not that they acquired that meaning from something else without administration. In that is the necessity of saying what we said. Or if something came to be from it or a part of it - not that it was hidden in something of what was mentioned - then the statement of the temporality of the world is necessitated by what is necessary in part of it. Then it is said to them: Since every observed thing has an end, and you made it a proof of the world, why is not the whole likewise? Otherwise...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative refutation by Imam al-Maturidi of several pre-Islamic theories that posited an eternal substrate or emanation for the created world. The passage first enumerates six erroneous views (enumerated as 2 through 6, with 1 presumably preceding on an earlier page). View 2 holds that things come from other things ad infinitum, driven by a wise originator who is the cause of the world, implying a cause without an effect. Maturidi points out that either that originator is not described with power and generosity in pre-eternity (a sign of need) or He is, in which case the effect and bestowal of goodness must be eternal—a self-contradiction. View 3 posits an eternal clay (al-ṭīna) as the original substance, with only the subsequent craft being temporal. Maturidi comments that this view arose from avoiding creation ex nihilo, but then all temporal things would arise from something else only through the transformation of the first substance, analogous to sperm and egg. View 4 attributes the world's origin to accidents that befell the eternal clay, causing it to transform into the various natures. View 5 attributes the origin directly to the Creator (al-Bārī) but likely still implies some form of emanation. View 6 calls the original substance 'hyle' (hayūlā). After listing these views, Maturidi summarizes their common core: they all attempt to repudiate what is not conceivable in imagination or representable in the mind—that is, creation from nothing—because they found the world existing and could not bear to affirm its coming from nothing. Maturidi then launches a dialectical refutation. He asks: 'Is it conceivable in your imaginations to repudiate what is not represented in the mind?' If they say yes, they are obstinate, because we all share the same faculty of imagination regarding things that have forms, and this repudiation of the non-imaginable is not itself imaginable. If they say no (i.e., it is merely a matter of estimation), then he asks: when is it conceivable in the imagination the pre-existence of a thing or its permanence after separation and its becoming imperceptible? They might accept such possibilities. Maturidi then points out that there are many things in the mind that are not representable to the senses, like hearing, sight, the flow of powers from one substance, and the generation of different powers from food. Therefore, one cannot deny by mere imagination the possibility of the world coming from nothing. He then argues more directly: the concept of 'something from something' must mean that the second thing was hidden in the first and then appeared. But this is impossible in many cases: a whole human being or tree cannot be hidden in a single sperm or seed. Even if one says it is hidden in substance, the substance cannot contain countless individuals. Furthermore, the human body in its entirety does not come from the sperm alone—it increases from food. But this increase cannot be attributed to the food itself, because food is lifeless and only produces growth through a wise administrator (tadbīr mudabbir ʿalīm). Thus, the argument of 'something from something' breaks down and actually proves the need for a Creator. Finally, Maturidi uses a reductio: if one accepts that every observed thing has a finite end (being temporal), and they use that as a proof for the world's temporality, then why is not the whole world also temporal? The argument concludes with the necessity of affirming creation from nothing by a wise Creator.

Key Terms
الوهم
النفس
المقدور
تدبير
مدبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'something from something' (e.g., creation from eternal matter) is a coherent explanation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this leads to absurdities like a whole human being hidden in a sperm, which is impossible. The only coherent explanation is creation from nothing by a wise Creator.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the refutation of the claim that something comes from something (like a human from sperm)?
A) It is possible because sperm contains all information.
B) It is impossible because the whole cannot be hidden in a part.
C) It is a matter of faith, not logic.
D) It is explained by food intake.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that creation ex nihilo is intellectually sound, while theories of eternal substrate collapse under logical scrutiny.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a logical proof that your own existence cannot be explained by natural processes alone—you were not hidden in your parents' cells. Do you live with the constant awareness that you are directly created by God, or do you let the routine of life make you forget this miracle? What will you do today to remind yourself that you are a direct act of the Creator?
?
Maturidi points out that our hearts struggle to accept creation from nothing. Is there a part of you that finds it easier to believe the world came from something else—like nature, chance, or time—rather than the direct command of God? What comfort does that false belief give you, and are you willing to surrender that comfort for the truth?
?
The diversity of outputs from the same food points to a wise Administrator over every detail of your body. How often do you thank God for the way your body processes food, or do you take it for granted? Will you pause before your next meal to acknowledge the Mudabbir who makes that transformation possible?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
Refuting Natural Causality for the Creator
Maturidi distinguishes two interpretations of 'cause' and refutes natural causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi rejects the idea that Allah is a 'cause' in the sense of natural necessity, and how he distinguishes between natural causality and free creation.

Core Idea
Allah is not a natural cause; He creates by will and power, not by necessity.

Imam Maturidi opens by addressing a claim that the Creator is the 'cause' of the world. He shows that if this means a natural cause (bil-tab'), it is absurd: nature produces uniformly, but the world is diverse and temporal. If it means He creates it, then the term 'cause' is inappropriate. This lesson establishes the crucial distinction between natural causality and divine free agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the Imam first states the claim and then offers two possible interpretations.
The passage is from Kitab al-Tawhid.
2
Identify the two meanings
Distinguish between 'cause by nature' (bi al-tab') and 'cause as creator' (yuhdith). Understand why the first is impossible and the second is correct but the term 'cause' is rejected.
The Arabic distinguishes between 'علة' and 'طبع'.
3
Grasp the reasoning
Reflect on the argument: natural cause yields uniformity, but the world is diverse and created. Thus, God cannot be a natural cause. This refutes any notion of divine necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وقوله : «الباري علة العالم، إن أراد به کون المصنوع به بالطبع فهو محال، لأنه طريق الاضطرار، ومن ذلك وصفه لا يُحتمل به كون العالم. على أن العالم مُحْدَث مختلف، ومَنْ كون الشيء به بالطبع فهو ذو نوع واحد. وإن أراد به أنه يُحدثه فذلك مستقيم، وتسميته علةً فاسدة. وذلك المعنى يوجب كون الشيء بعد أن لم يكن لأوجه. أحدها التناقض، إذ لا عدم يوجد، فتقع الحاجة إلى من يوجده، فثبت أن في ذلك وجوب كونه حادثا. والثاني كون كلية العالم به، ومعلوم كون الحادث بعد أن لم يكن. والله أعلم. والثالث أن في ذلك وجود الاجتماع مع التفرق، والحركة مع السكون، والحياة مع الموت، وفي ذلك تناقض وتناف. ثبت أنه كان على التتابع بالأول والثاني ونحوه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And his statement: 'The Creator is the cause of the world' — if he means by that the existence of the effect by nature, then it is impossible, because it is the way of necessity, and from that description it cannot be that the world comes to be. Moreover, the world is created and diverse, while one who makes a thing by nature has only one type. But if he means that He creates it, then that is correct, but calling Him 'cause' is corrupt. And that meaning necessitates that a thing comes to be after it was not, for reasons: First, contradiction, because non-existence does not exist, so the need arises for one who brings it into existence. Thus it is established that in that is the necessity of its being created. Second, the entirety of the world is through Him, and it is known that the created comes to be after it was not. And Allah knows best. Third, in that is the existence of union with separation, motion with rest, life with death, and in that is contradiction and opposition. It is established that it was in succession, by the first and second and the like. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that God is the 'cause' ('illa) of the world. Maturidi distinguishes two meanings: if 'cause' means a natural cause (bil-tab'), that is impossible because it implies necessity and would make the world uniform, while the world is diverse and temporal. If it means that God creates the world (yuhdithuhu), then the term 'cause' is inappropriate. He then gives three arguments (wujuh) showing that the world must have a beginning. First, if the world were eternal, non-existence would never occur, but we observe things coming into existence, so there must be a creator. Second, the entire world is through Him, and created things are known to exist after non-existence. Third, the world contains opposites like union and separation, motion and rest, life and death, which cannot coexist, so the world must have been created in succession (on the first and second, etc.).

Key Terms
علة
طبع
اضطرار
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think God's creation is like natural causation, where the effect follows necessarily from the cause.
Correction: Maturidi refutes this, showing that natural causality implies uniformity and necessity, but the world is diverse and contingent, so God cannot be a natural cause. Divine creation is free and by will.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if 'cause' means natural causality, what is the problem?
A) It is metaphysically impossible.
B) It would make the world uniform, but it is diverse.
C) It denies God's existence.
D) It is the correct view.

Key Takeaway
Allah is not a natural cause; He creates freely by His will, not by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's actions in your life, do you ever feel like He is bound by a system — that He must reward you if you do X or punish if you do Y? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? What step can you take today to trust that Allah acts by His free will, not by your calculations?
?
The world is diverse and originated — a sign of Allah's creative will. Have you ever looked at the diversity of creation and felt awe at His freedom? Or do you take it for granted as mere 'natural' processes? Commit today to reflecting on one aspect of creation that shows Allah's will, not natural necessity.
?
Maturidi warns against using the term 'cause' for Allah because it implies necessity. Are there words or phrases you use about Allah that carry unintended implications? (e.g., 'God is the cause of everything' without qualification). How can you speak about Allah's actions with more precision and reverence starting today?

Three Proofs That the World Has a Beginning
Maturidi gives three arguments for the world's beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three arguments that the world must have been created from non-existence, refuting the view that it is eternal.

Core Idea
The world's diversity and opposites prove it is created and not eternal.

After rejecting the notion of natural causality, Imam Maturidi provides three reasons why the world must have a beginning. These arguments are based on contradiction, divine agency, and the coexistence of opposites. This lesson walks through each argument to solidify the doctrine of the world's temporal creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage containing the three arguments. Note the structure: 'أحدها... والثاني... والثالث...'
The passage is the continuation from the previous lesson.
2
Examine the first argument
The first argument is based on contradiction: if the world were eternal, non-existence would never occur, but we observe things coming into existence, so a creator is needed.
Maturidi says: 'إذ لا عدم يوجد، فتقع الحاجة إلى من يوجده'.
3
Examine the second and third arguments
The second argument is that the entire world is through Him, and the created exists after non-existence. The third argument notes the coexistence of opposites (union/separation, motion/rest, life/death) which cannot be simultaneous, so the world must have been created in succession.
These arguments strengthen the conclusion that the world is created and not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وقوله : «الباري علة العالم، إن أراد به کون المصنوع به بالطبع فهو محال، لأنه طريق الاضطرار، ومن ذلك وصفه لا يُحتمل به كون العالم. على أن العالم مُحْدَث مختلف، ومَنْ كون الشيء به بالطبع فهو ذو نوع واحد. وإن أراد به أنه يُحدثه فذلك مستقيم، وتسميته علةً فاسدة. وذلك المعنى يوجب كون الشيء بعد أن لم يكن لأوجه. أحدها التناقض، إذ لا عدم يوجد، فتقع الحاجة إلى من يوجده، فثبت أن في ذلك وجوب كونه حادثا. والثاني كون كلية العالم به، ومعلوم كون الحادث بعد أن لم يكن. والله أعلم. والثالث أن في ذلك وجود الاجتماع مع التفرق، والحركة مع السكون، والحياة مع الموت، وفي ذلك تناقض وتناف. ثبت أنه كان على التتابع بالأول والثاني ونحوه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And his statement: 'The Creator is the cause of the world' — if he means by that the existence of the effect by nature, then it is impossible, because it is the way of necessity, and from that description it cannot be that the world comes to be. Moreover, the world is created and diverse, while one who makes a thing by nature has only one type. But if he means that He creates it, then that is correct, but calling Him 'cause' is corrupt. And that meaning necessitates that a thing comes to be after it was not, for reasons: First, contradiction, because non-existence does not exist, so the need arises for one who brings it into existence. Thus it is established that in that is the necessity of its being created. Second, the entirety of the world is through Him, and it is known that the created comes to be after it was not. And Allah knows best. Third, in that is the existence of union with separation, motion with rest, life with death, and in that is contradiction and opposition. It is established that it was in succession, by the first and second and the like. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that God is the 'cause' ('illa) of the world. Maturidi distinguishes two meanings: if 'cause' means a natural cause (bil-tab'), that is impossible because it implies necessity and would make the world uniform, while the world is diverse and temporal. If it means that God creates the world (yuhdithuhu), then the term 'cause' is inappropriate. He then gives three arguments (wujuh) showing that the world must have a beginning. First, if the world were eternal, non-existence would never occur, but we observe things coming into existence, so there must be a creator. Second, the entire world is through Him, and created things are known to exist after non-existence. Third, the world contains opposites like union and separation, motion and rest, life and death, which cannot coexist, so the world must have been created in succession (on the first and second, etc.).

Key Terms

تفرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the world is eternal and does not need a creator.
Correction: Maturidi's arguments show that the world must have a beginning: it contains contingencies and opposites that require a creator and a temporal origin.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument Maturidi gives for the world's createdness?
A) The world is harmonious.
B) Non-existence does not exist, so a creator is needed for things that come into existence.
C) The world is complex.
D) The world has a purpose.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi provides three reasons why the world must have been created: contradiction, dependence on God, and the coexistence of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything in your life—your health, your wealth, your relationships—is created after non-existence, why do you still hold onto them as if they are permanent? What would change in your daily priorities if you truly believed that your very existence is constantly brought into being by the Creator?
?
Can you identify one area of your life where you have been acting as if it is eternal—something you take for granted without acknowledging its createdness? What concrete step will you take today to remind yourself that it is a gift from God that could vanish at any moment?
?
The third proof mentions opposites like life and death. If you are alive now, you will inevitably face death. How does this undeniable truth shape the way you use the time you have been given? Are there actions or habits you need to change because you have been living as if death is far away?
